    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ed709ddda97c47ca964077c0e86a5c33">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9eaf6103-6992-4e13-bdd5-32cb9bf33710'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhang/Qi">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Tang/Yougen" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yougen Tang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Yougen Tang</p>
                     <p>Hunan Provincial Key Laboratory of Chemical Power Sources, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Central South University, Changsha, 410083 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Tang/Yougen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Qiu/Tian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Tian Qiu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-name">Tian Qiu</p>
                     <p>Guangdong DFP New Material Group Co. LTD, Shantou, 515064 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Qiu/Tian">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wang/Haiyan" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a10" data-db-target-for="a10" aria-controls="a10" aria-haspopup="true" id="a10_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Haiyan Wang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a10" aria-labelledby="a10_Ctrl" role="region" id="a10">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d176591732" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anode-free sodium batteries (AFSBs) are a desirable choice for high-energy-density and low-cost battery systems. However, the rapid capacity decline resulting from irreversible sodium plating and stripping remains a big challenge for AFSBs. Herein, a copper-dominated Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb composite coating employing a co-sputtering strategy is designed to improve the sodophilicity of copper foils. The Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb coating can construct a durably sodophilic and stable interface by suppressing the alloying transition of the sodophilic phase and alleviating the volumetric deformation of the coating. This stable alloy interface can greatly enhance sodium nucleation and improve ion transport at the interface by forming homogeneous NaF-rich solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. Ultimately, the AFSB assembled using this unique substrate with a highly loaded Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode (≈10&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) can cycle 600&nbsp;cycles deliver a discharge capacity of 75.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 74.2%, which is much higher than those of the untreated one or antimony coating one (&lt;30% after 200&nbsp;cycles). This co-sputtering strategy provides new insights into the design of sodophilic coatings with sodium-alloying ability and can be extended to the optimization of zinc-metal batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400367-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries have garnered attention in the energy storage industry due to their advantages over widely used lithium-ion batteries in terms of resource reserves, manufacturing cost, and safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0001_R_d176591533e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The components of sodium-ion batteries, including cathode materials, electrolytes, and diaphragm materials, have been rapidly developed benefiting from the advancements in the lithium battery industry.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0002_R_d176591533e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the main current impediment to the development of sodium batteries is widely acknowledged to be the anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0003_R_d176591533e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano J Cent South Univ">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon is a commercially promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries, but it also suffers from relatively low capacity, high cost, and low initial Coulombic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0004_R_d176591533e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium metal anode has a high theoretical capacity (1166&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<b><sup>−</sup></b><sup>1</sup>) and low redox potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs SHE), which can be used to construct sodium metal batteries with high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0005_R_d176591533e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To achieve longer cycle life, sodium metal batteries often use excessive sodium metal, which sacrifices some of the energy density of the battery. At the same time, the production process of the sodium metal anode severely limits the development of sodium metal batteries, as it is very difficult and incompatible with existing lithium battery manufacture.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0006_R_d176591533e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Anode-free sodium batteries (AFSBs) surpass conventional sodium batteries in termsof energy density, manufacturing simplicity, process safety, and overall battery cost due to its innovative utilization of a current collector as the anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0007_R_d176591533e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - iScience">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The AFSBs have attracted the interest of many researchers and hold great promise for future applications in the high specific energy, low-cost battery market.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0007_R_d176591533e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - iScience">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, in comparison to sodium metal batteries, the AFSBs encounter more severe challenge of poor cycle life, primarily arising from the absence of active sodium to replenish the irreversible loss of sodium ions caused by the dead sodium and irreversible SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0007_R_d176591533e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - iScience">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0008_R_d176591533e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Constructing a thin and dense SEI through electrolyte design is an effective method to reduce the irreversible loss of sodium metal and enhance the cycling stability of AFSBs. For instance, a boron-containing SEI was designed to suppress the side reaction of sodium metal at the crack by adding NaBF<sub>4</sub> to the ether-based electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0006_R_d176591533e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mixed solvents with different chain ethers were also utilized to form a high-voltage stable electrolyte. More recently, to replenish irreversible sodium loss and construct a stable SEI film synergistically, sodium compensation additives, like Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, have been developed for achieving better cycle life of AFSBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0009_R_d176591533e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J Am Chem Soc">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Designing a homogeneously artificial layer at the substrate surface can improve the cycling stability of AFSBs, in addition to the sodium metal surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0010_R_d176591533e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The efficiency of sodium ion transport at the interface was obviously enhanced by modifying a layer of sodium formate (artificial SEI) on the surface of copper foil using the hydrothermal method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0011_R_d176591533e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ultimately, a AFSB assembled with this optimized copper foil can deliver a capacity retention of 88.2% after 400&nbsp;cycles. As well known, increasing the sodophilicity of the substrate can enhance the uniformity of sodium deposition and stripping processes in the initial cycles, thereby effectively inhibiting the formation of sodium dendrites and dead sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0008_R_d176591533e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, copper-carbon composite,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0012_R_d176591533e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> fluorinated porous framework,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0013_R_d176591533e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Sci. Adv.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> nitrogen-tin doped mesoporous carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0014_R_d176591533e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and carbon-shell-coated iron nanoparticles,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0015_R_d176591533e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can provide sufficient nucleation sites for sodium deposition. Nevertheless, designing sodophilic and stable interfaces or artificial SEIs by a low-cost and scalable method still faces big challenges. In sodium metal batteries, sodophilic metal coatings (e.g., Sn, Te, Sb, Ga, In, and Bi easily forming alloys with sodium) have been widely reported, and sodium can easily nucleate and grow uniformly on these coatings, thus increasing the cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0016_R_d176591533e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - eScience eScience Adv. Mater. Energy Stor. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These metals can be sputtered onto the surface by a simple and scalable magnetron sputtering process, which shows significant application potential in the modification of collectors for AFSBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0017_R_d176591533e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these metal coatings will easily alloy with sodium accompanied by big volumetric change, leading to unstable interfacial structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0018_R_d176591533e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the sodophilic coatings may lose their initial sodophilic sites following an irreversible sodium-alloying transition, leading to a diminished ability to modulate sodium plating/stripping by the coatings.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0019_R_d176591533e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taking these factors into account, we posit that when designing sodophilic interfaces with alloying capabilities, it is essential to prioritize the volumetric deformation, capacity, and reversibility of the coatings during sodium-alloying process, thereby preventing the recurrent formation of irreversible SEI films and the loss of the original sodophilic sites.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, by a combination of in situ/ex situ characterization experiments and theoretical calculations, we first revealed that the accumulation of dead sodium and irreversible SEI due to the insufficient sodophilicity and interface stability of the substrate was an essential cause of rapid capacity decay of AFSBs. Accordingly, a Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating strategy was proposed by a facile co-sputtering strategy to enhance the sodophilicity and interface stability of the substrate. In contrast to the unstable Sb coating, Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating with low antimony content could retain the sodophilic Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb(2 0 0) facet and form a low-expansion and irreversible sodium-alloy during cycling, which maintained the structural stability and sodophilicity of the coatings and induced the formation of thin and dense fluorinated SEI films. Ultimately, this ultra-thin Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating significantly enhanced the reversibility of sodium deposition and stripping, enabling stable cycling of AFSB over 600&nbsp;cycles. Note that this interfacial modification strategy can be available for the zinc-metal anode, realizing long-life, and dendrite-free zinc-metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400367-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400367-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation and Characterization of Sodophilic Coating</h3>
            
            <p>To improve the sodophilicity of commercial copper foils, we used a dual-target magnetron sputtering technique to deposit a metallic coating of copper and antimony on the rough surface of copper foil. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1a,b</a>, the surface of the copper foil is uniformly covered with nanoparticles of copper and antimony with a thickness of ≈180&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S5</a>, Supporting Information). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) results show that a Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy phase was formed via co-sputtering (denoted as Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu), while the sputtering of a single antimony target onto a copper foil resulted in pure antimony phase formation (denoted as Sb@Cu) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1d</a>). We deposited metal with identical compositions onto molybdenum mesh microgrids to conduct an in-depth investigation of the crystal structure of the coatings. The sputtered copper coating using a pure copper target consists of well-crystallized copper nanoparticles with a size of ≈12&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). For the antimony coating with a pure antimony target, it comprises poorly crystallized nanoparticles, and its electron diffraction circles are unsharp (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1g,h</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). When using co-sputtering strategy, well-crystallized copper phase and Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy phase can be achieved (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1i,j</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The dominant crystal facets of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb phase are (0 0 3) and (2 0 0) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1k</a>). The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) in transmission electron microscopy (TEM) region indicates that the alloy coating contains 87.8% copper atoms and 12.2% antimony atoms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1l</a>). The metal antimony will form antimony-sodium alloys during sodium deposition, whereas copper will not form copper-sodium alloys with sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0016_R_d176591533e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - eScience eScience Adv. Mater. Energy Stor. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0020_R_d176591533e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Acta Phys. Chim. Sin.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is clear that the low concentration of antimony in the coating can alleviate structural expansion due to the formation of antimony-sodium alloys, and the high content of copper acts as a conductive framework to maintain the structural stability of the coating during cycling. In this study, adjusting the elemental composition of multi-component coatings can be easily achieved by varying the sputtering power of different metal targets. This co-sputtering strategy can also be employed for the fabrication of Cu–Sn composite coatings. However, owing to the larger size of tin particles, Cu–Sn alloy phase cannot be well formed, instead, tin crystals are observed along with copper particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1m,n</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The interactions between the sodium atoms with various metal facets were assessed through density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The predominant facets in the substrate were identified based on XRD and TEM test results, and their binding energy with sodium was subsequently computed. The binding energy of sodium adsorbed on Cu(2 0 0), Cu(1 1 1), Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb (0 0 3), Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb (2 0 0), Sb(0 0 3), Sb(1 0 4), and Sn(1 0 1) is calculated to be −2.75, −2.8, −2.89, −3.67, −5.27, −4.6, and −6.08&nbsp;eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0001">1o</a>). As seen, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating exhibits much higher sodophilicity compared to copper, which is anticipated to enhance the reversibility of sodium plating/stripping at the copper foil interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0015_R_d176591533e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, tin and antimony, which possess sodium alloying capability, also demonstrate a strong affinity for sodium. Throughout the sodium alloying process, the tin (or antimony) undergoes a substantial expansion in volume, posing a critical constraint on the maintenance of the structural stability of the coating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0003_R_d176591533e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano J Cent South Univ">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, this volume expansion can be effectively mitigated by decreasing the tin (or antimony) content within the composite coating. Therefore, the multi-target co-sputtering technique enables the creation of uniform sodophilic sites on the substrate's surface through the formation of sodophilic alloying phases or the physical dilution/dispersion of sodophilic materials. More importantly, this technique can well address the challenge of high volumetric expansion of sodophilic components in coatings undergoing sodium-alloying reactions during sodium deposition/exfoliation in AFSBs.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400367-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ee23047-14cc-4b22-9038-9423827467ed/aenm202400367-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ee23047-14cc-4b22-9038-9423827467ed/aenm202400367-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1d8363c0-7747-41ad-b183-f88e53ef0c78/aenm202400367-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ee23047-14cc-4b22-9038-9423827467ed/aenm202400367-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400367-sec-0040-title">2.2 Investigation of Sodium Deposition Morphology and Stripping Reversibility</h3>
            
            <p>The in situ optical image evolution of sodium deposition visually illustrates that copper foil performs disordered dendrite growth due to insufficient nucleation (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2a</a>; Movie <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), whereas sodophilic Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu achieves dendrite-free sodium deposition owing to uniform and dense sodium nucleation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2b</a>; Movie <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Adequate nucleation facilitates the formation of a more extensive array of contact sites with the substrate during sodium exfoliation, thereby enhancing the efficiency of sodium stripping on the substrate. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the half-cells demonstrate an initial nucleation overpotential of 12&nbsp;mV for sodium on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate, which is lower than that observed on copper foil (19&nbsp;mV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2c</a>). The reduced nucleation overpotential suggests a higher propensity for sodium nucleation on Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu compared to copper foil. Furthermore, a superior overlap of CV curves further implies enhanced sodium plating/stripping reversibility for Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0021_R_d176591533e744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, no alloying reaction peak is noted for Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu during cycling, while Sb@Cu has an obvious alloying-dealloying reaction peak near 0.7&nbsp;V with relatively poor reversibility (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the sodium alloying transition of antimony is greatly restricted upon the formation of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb. Therefore, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate can maintain good structural stability and sustained sodophilicity during cycling. Then, we characterized the initial morphology of the substrate after deposition of 0.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> sodium in a coin-cell. When the substrate is not fully covered by sodium, it becomes feasible to accurately characterize both the number and size of the sodium crystal nucleus. The SEM images show that the sodium nuclei on the copper foil are relatively few and fail to cover the surface adequately (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), whereas those on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu surface are more numerous and spread evenly on the substrate surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2f</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). At a sodium deposition capacity of 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, the SEM images of surface can no longer discern the deposition differences between the various substrates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information) because of the complete coverage by metallic sodium. However, upon cutting the copper foil and Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu after sodium deposition with a cryo-focused ion beam, it is observed that the contact surface of Cu foil with sodium has several holes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2e</a>), while Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu with sodium is tightly connected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2g</a>). This secure junction is attributed to the enrichment of the sodium nucleation sites, providing an abundant electronic pathway for the subsequent sodium stripping process. In situ XRD was employed to detect dead sodium production during sodium plating/stripping in half-cells at 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for 0.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2h,i</a>, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate has no residual sodium signal after sodium stripping, while the Cu foil has a strong residual Na(1 1 0) signal, which implies that the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu can effectively avoid the formation of dead sodium, while the copper foil generates a large amount of dead sodium or low-active sodium. The dead sodium generated during cycling will directly lead to capacity decay due to insufficient discharge. The capacity-voltage curves of the half-cells indicate that Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu has a higher initial plating/stripping efficiency of 98.68% than Cu foil (96.33%). Additionally, Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu exhibits a smaller voltage hysteresis than Cu foil. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0002">2j</a>). However, the Sb@Cu exhibits a lower initial plating/stripping efficiency due to the loss of active-sodium from the poorly reversible sodium alloying reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Generally, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate prepared using the co-sputtering technique, not only demonstrates favorable sodophilicity but also prevents antimony from undergoing an alloying transition, resulting in excellent reversibility of sodium plating/stripping.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400367-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13e1c5c6-9603-4f3f-aa86-1410f5c71db5/aenm202400367-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/13e1c5c6-9603-4f3f-aa86-1410f5c71db5/aenm202400367-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70a12622-9a48-4e6c-a326-ada7066963f3/aenm202400367-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13e1c5c6-9603-4f3f-aa86-1410f5c71db5/aenm202400367-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400367-sec-0050-title">2.3 Stabilization Mechanism of Alloy Coating</h3>
            
            <p>The XRD was employed to investigate the crystalline phase transformation of the coatings after sodium deposition/exfoliation. As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3a</a>, Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloy and NaCu<sub>4</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> alloy are formed on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate after the initial sodium deposition, and a higher amount of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb is retained, whereas all the antimony on the Sb@Cu substrate is transferred to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloy. The presence of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate may result from a few antimony nanocrystals that could not establish a stable alloying phase with copper, and thus easily formed Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloys with sodium. The NaCu<sub>4</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> originates from the sodium alloy transition of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb(0 0 3) facet, while the stable Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb(2 0 0) facet retains its original state without forming a sodium-alloy. During the initial de-sodiumation stage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), Na<sub>3</sub>Sb on the Sb@Cu substrate is transformed to antimony and NaSb alloy, while the crystal phases on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu remain well and do not experience a reversible transformation. The crystal structures of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu surface retain the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (2 0 1), NaCu<sub>4</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> (1 1 0), and Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb (2 0 0) components even after 20 and 100&nbsp;cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, antimony on Sb@Cu substrate forms the NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloys after experiencing several less reversible crystalline phase transitions. To further investigate the structural change in the coating during cycling, a half-cell was assembled by employing a molybdenum mesh microgrid sputtered with nano-coating (Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb or Sb). After several sodium plating/stripping, the molybdenum mesh was extracted and used for TEM observation subsequently. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3c–e</a>, the composite coating consisting of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy and copper can maintain good crystallinity and structural stability. Sodophilic Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb(2 0 0) is well maintained after 5&nbsp;cycles as observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), which can durably modulate efficient sodium nucleation during cycling as highly sodophilic sites. The composite alloy coating exhibits exceptional structural stability even after 20&nbsp;cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, the antimony coating is still composed of poorly crystallized sodium-antimony alloys and metallic antimony (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). The sodium content in the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb composite coating after cycling is 16.4%, which is much lower than that of 62.7% in the Sb coating (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3f,h</a>). The high sodium content means that the antimony coating consumes more active sodium, thus causing more irreversible capacity loss. Further, we simulated the volume expansion during different sodium-alloying processes. As can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3i</a>, the volume expansion of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb transformed to NaCu<sub>4</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> alloy is 35.2%, which is much lower than those of antimony transformed to NaSb (86.6%) and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (151%). Therefore, Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb–Cu composite coating with low antimony content can maintain excellent structural stability during cycling owing to the low-expansion irreversible sodium-alloy transition. In contrast, the antimony coating needs to undergo alloy transformations with large volume changes repeatedly, posing a big challenge to interface stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0022_R_d176591533e981" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The influence of the differences in sodophilicity and structural stability of the substrate on sodium deposition/exfoliation can be reflected in the Coulombic efficiency and nucleation curves of the half-cells. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3j</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information), the copper foil has a high and unstable nucleation overpotential during cycling, which can be attributed to the shielding of the nucleation site by the unstable dead-sodium, and the insufficient sodophilicity of the substrate. Similarly, the nucleation overpotential gradually rises, and the Coulombic efficiency becomes unstable as the cycle number increases for the Sb@Cu substrate. Satisfactorily, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate with durable sodophilicity and structural stability can maintain a minimal nucleation overpotential and an exceptionally stable voltage hysteresis during the long-term cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0003">3k</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400367-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e30beeff-06ee-467d-8bc9-e7646b1d2713/aenm202400367-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e30beeff-06ee-467d-8bc9-e7646b1d2713/aenm202400367-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5ac1c669-c142-4a65-a91e-d046a7694461/aenm202400367-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e30beeff-06ee-467d-8bc9-e7646b1d2713/aenm202400367-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400367-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400367" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of the initial sodiumation of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu and Sb@Cu (half-cell with a discharge cut-off voltage of 0&nbsp;V). b) XRD patterns of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu and Sb@Cu in the stripped state after 20&nbsp;cycles (half-cell with a charge cut-off voltage of 0.5&nbsp;V). c,d) TEM images, e) electron diffraction image, and f) EDS mappings of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy particles on molybdenum mesh after 5&nbsp;cycles (in the stripped state). g) TEM image and electron diffraction image, and h) EDS mappings of antimony layer on molybdenum mesh after 5&nbsp;cycles (in the stripped state). i) Volume expansion of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb and antimony in different sodium-alloy states. j) Coulombic efficiency of the Cu||Na, Sb@Cu||Na, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu||Na half-cells. k) The voltage-time curves of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu||Na half-cell at 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400367-sec-0060-title">2.4 The Evolution of Sodium Plating/Stripping Morphology and SEI Structure</h3>
            
            <p>SEM and time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) were employed to investigate in depth the evolution of sodium plating/stripping morphology and SEI structure in AFSBs. The specialized transfer device was utilized during the characterization process to prevent the electrodes from being exposed to air. The full cells assembled using the as-prepared substrate as an anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) with a mass loading of ≈10&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> as cathode were used to characterize the sodium plating/stripping morphology after cycling. Commercially available NVP electrodes can cycle stably and maintain a high discharge-specific capacity with high loading and current density (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information), so the capacity decay of AFSBs is mainly dependent on the anode contribution. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4a</a>, a large amount of dead sodium remains on the anode side of the anode-free Cu||NVP cell, indicating that the Cu||NVP cannot be discharged sufficiently (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). The accumulation of dead sodium during cycling shields the nucleation sites that are required for subsequent sodium deposition, thereby inducing loose and inhomogeneous sodium deposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). Time of flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) was performed to characterize the spatial distribution of sodium ions and anions at the substrate interface after cycling. It is well known that the SEI films on anode consist predominantly of sodium ions and anions, both of which possess TOF-SIMS signals, so the structural information about SEI can be derived by identifying the signals of sodium ions and anions. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4c–e</a>, the copper foil surface exhibits porous dead sodium and uneven distribution of Na ions, which will accelerate the capacity decay of the anode-free battery due to the low efficiency and non-uniformity of electron/ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0009_R_d176591533e1080" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J Am Chem Soc">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast to the insufficiently sodophilic copper foils, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate exhibits negligible dead-sodium residue after 50&nbsp;cycles and maintains the structural stability of the coating (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). The sodium is still deposited uniformly on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu surface as spherical in deposition process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). The efficient stripping and ordered plating can be attributed to the stable Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb composite coating, which forms a durably sodophilic interface. This interface consistently provides nucleation sites for sodium deposition, thereby enhancing the electron/ion exchange behavior at the interface. The TOF-SIMS results indicate that a thin and homogeneous SEI is formed after cycling on Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4h–j</a>). However, the structural integrity of the initially sodophilic Sb@Cu is disrupted and the sodophilic antimony sites are removed after undergoing repeated alloy transformations, thus inducing the formation of dead sodium (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4k</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). The loss of a homogeneous sodophilic interface is followed by a significant reduction of sodium nucleation sites on Sb@Cu, resulting in the formation of uneven sodium deposits (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4l</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a>, Supporting Information). It should be noted that a thick and inhomogeneous SEI film is formed on Sb@Cu due to the repeated alloying transition with large volume change, which is another essential factor for sodium failure to nucleate sufficiently during the cycling process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4m–p</a>). Digital photographs of the post-cycling electrodes similarly demonstrate that Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrates are more susceptible to performing efficient sodium stripping without dead sodium compared to copper foil and Sb@Cu substrates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information). More importantly, a thin and dense fluorinated electrolyte film is formed on the surface of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu during the cycling process, which also contains a few organic components (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0004">4p–s</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0023_R_d176591533e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) curves also provide important evidence for the formation of a dense NaF layer on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a>, Supporting Information). As well known, a homogeneous fluorinated SEI interface is critical for the rapid transport of sodium ions, which can obviously improve the efficiency and homogeneity of sodium nucleation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0024_R_d176591533e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Environ. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the dead sodium and thick irreversible SEIs, which are induced by insufficient sodophilicity and unstable interfacial structure, are critical causes of the capacity decay for AFSBs. Herein, the unique Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate with a durably sodophilic interface, which can maintain the structural stability and form a homogeneous fluorinated SEI during cycling, is expected to solve the challenge of the low reversibility of AFSBs.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400367-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a90bc4d5-e655-4fb6-816a-03f1520f85cb/aenm202400367-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a90bc4d5-e655-4fb6-816a-03f1520f85cb/aenm202400367-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/43f1bc21-5dd2-467b-8106-55d6158b1dd8/aenm202400367-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a90bc4d5-e655-4fb6-816a-03f1520f85cb/aenm202400367-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400367-sec-0070-title">2.5 The Electrochemical Performance of Anode-Free Sodium Battery</h3>
            
            <p>The AFSBs were assembled to further demonstrate the practicality of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating. It should be noted that the electrodes used in the AFSBs have not been subjected to any pre-sodiumation treatment that might have an influence on the battery capacity. As indicated, the AFSBs assembled with structurally unstable Sb@Cu and insufficiently sodophilic copper foil display low initial Coulombic efficiencies (82.3% and 82.9%, respectively) and rapid capacity decay at 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0005">5a,b</a>). Encouragingly, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu||NVP cell exhibits an initial Coulombic efficiency of 87.5% and a discharge specific capacity of 79.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300&nbsp;cycles. When the current density is increased to 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it can still deliver a discharge capacity of 75.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 600&nbsp;cycles with a capacity retention of 74.2% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0005">5c,d</a>), indicating outstanding cycling stability. The AFSB optimized by this simple and scalable alloy sputtering strategy exhibits significant advantages in terms of cycle life with low decay rate compared to previously reported work (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Conversely, the specific capacity of Sb@Cu||NVP rapidly decays from 90.2 to 30&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200&nbsp;cycles. This rapid capacity decay should be attributed to the formation of thick SEI and dead sodium that is caused by the structural instability and the destructive transition of sodophilic sites for the antimony coating. Similarly, the discharge capacity of the AFSB assembled with copper foil decreases from 89 to 28.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200&nbsp;cycles. The insufficient sodophilicity induces the formation of dead sodium due to inadequate contact between sodium and the substrate, thereby reducing the electron transport pathway during sodium stripping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0025_R_d176591533e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J Energy Chem">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high specific surface area of dead sodium accelerates side reactions at the sodium-electrolyte interface, leading to the formation of a thick and irreversible SEI films, which will further deteriorate the cycling stability of AFSBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0026_R_d176591533e1263" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0005">5e</a>, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate also delivers superior rate performance and can be cycled stably for more than 350&nbsp;cycles at a current density of 800&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, thanks to the durably sodophilic interface and the thin, dense fluorinated SEI film (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0005">5f</a>). The CV curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu||NVP cell also demonstrate that the alloying transition of the alloy coating is greatly suppressed, thus enhancing the reversibility of sodium deposition/stripping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast to the stable Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate, the Sb@Cu experiences a reversible alloying transition repeatedly, which impedes the formation of a stable SEI film and removes the initial antimony sodophilic sites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a>, Supporting Information). The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) results of the AFSBs also indicate that the electron-ion transport impedance of the insufficiently sodophilic and structurally unstable substrates increases significantly after repeated sodium plating/stripping, whereas the stable and sodophilic Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu is able to essentially maintain the initial level (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400367-fig-0005">5g</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0027_R_d176591533e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Phys. Chem. C">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Practically, commercial aluminum foils modified with Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating also show excellent cycling stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a>, Supporting Information). The Cu-Sn composite coatings prepared using the co-sputtering strategy, can also enhance the cycling stability of AFSBs by reducing the amount of tin in the coating, thereby mitigating volume expansion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a>, Supporting Information). However, maintaining the durable sodophilicity of Cu-Sn@Cu is challenging since copper and tin metals did not form a homogeneous copper-tin alloy to suppress the conversion of the sodophilic tin to a sodium-tin alloy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0028_R_d176591533e1312" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As seen in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a> (Supporting Information), some dead sodium is present on the surface of the Cu-Sn@Cu substrate after cycling, but the uniformity of sodium deposition is improved to a certain extent since the structure of the coating remains intact. In summary, highly stable interface and durable sodophilicity of the coatings can be achieved by modulating the interfacial alloying transition behavior through a facile and scalable co-sputtering strategy.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400367-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2176a52d-ffa7-4323-9e4a-62faa00a4d16/aenm202400367-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2176a52d-ffa7-4323-9e4a-62faa00a4d16/aenm202400367-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/74744c26-aacc-4e37-a73e-ef310a46655e/aenm202400367-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2176a52d-ffa7-4323-9e4a-62faa00a4d16/aenm202400367-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400367-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400367" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cyclic stability and capacity-voltage curves of Cu||NVP, Sb@Cu||NVP, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu||NVP anode-free cells at a,b) 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and c,d) 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. e,f) Rate performance and the corresponding capacity-voltage curves of Cu||NVP and Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu||NVP anode-free cells. g) The EIS of AFSBs in a fully charged state after different cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202400367-sec-0080-title">2.6 Alloy Coating Strategy for Zinc-Metal Battery</h3>
            
            <p>We note that copper and antimony also have good zincophilicity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0029_R_d176591533e1361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and this co-sputtering alloy strategy may be equally applicable to zinc metal batteries for solving the zinc dendrite problem. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41</a> (Supporting Information), the surface of the zinc foil has been coated with a layer of nano-Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy. The uniform Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb coating demonstrates a mitigating effect on the corrosion of zinc foil when exposed to a ZnSO<sub>4</sub> solution (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42</a>, Supporting Information). More importantly, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb coating can provide rich nucleation sites for zinc deposition, which enables obtaining uniform zinc deposits by refining zinc grains (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43</a>, Supporting Information). Nevertheless, the bare zinc forms zinc dendrites during deposition since it lacks nucleation sites. These dendrites pose a risk of diaphragm puncture, potentially causing short circuit in the battery (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44</a>, Supporting Information). The Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu also exhibits higher Coulombic efficiency, longer cycle life, and lower voltage hysteresis during cycling when compared to copper foil (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45</a>, Supporting Information). The symmetrical cell assembled with Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Zn displays a cycle life of more than 800&nbsp;h at 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> owing to the important role of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb coating in regulating zinc nucleation and inhibiting zinc corrosion (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S46</a>, Supporting Information). The Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Zn demonstrates extended cycling life and reduced voltage hysteresis even at 4.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> for 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S47</a>, Supporting Information). The zinc metal batteries were assembled with the as-prepared zinc anodes and the NH<sub>4</sub>V<sub>4</sub>O<sub>10</sub> cathode (prepared using the previously reported method).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400367-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400367-bib-0030_R_d176591533e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Research">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The full cell using Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Zn anode exhibits the best cycling performance with the discharge capacity of 126.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000&nbsp;cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S48</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the ultra-thin Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating strategy here can not only significantly improve the cycling stability of AFSBs, but also extend to the interface modification of zinc-metal anodes, enabling long-life and dendrite-free metal batteries.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400367-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a durably sodophilic Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating was developed by a simple co-sputtering strategy to overcome the poor reversibility challenge of AFSBs. The sodophilic coatings could establish effective electron/ion conduction pathways for highly reversible sodium plating/stripping through abundant nucleation contact sites. However, the inhomogeneous SEI arising from the structural instability of sodophilic coating was anticipated to diminish the efficiency of electron/ion transfer at the interface and further exacerbate the formation of dead sodium by shielding the pristine nucleation sites on the substrate. For instance, the antimony coating with initial sodophilicity involved repeated alloy transitions with large volume change during cycling, thereby failing to form a stable interface. In contrast, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb@Cu substrate with low antimony content could maintain excellent structural stability and durable sodophilicity, thus forming a homogeneous fluorinated SEI film during cycling owing to the suppression of sodium-alloy transitions. Ultimately, the AFSBs modified with a Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb alloy coating showed capacity retention of 74.2% after 600&nbsp;cycles at 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is far higher than those with antimony coating. More importantly, this work is the first to propose the use of a co-sputtering alloy strategy to construct structurally stable and durably sodophilic interfaces by regulating the extent and reversibility of sodium-alloying transformations. This strategy provides important guidance for the design of highly reversible anode-free sodium batteries and can be extended to zinc-metal batteries as well.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

               <h1></h1>
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202400367-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h1>Data Availability Statement</h1>

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
